These issues were mitigated through years of therapy with Davis, to whom he was engaged at the time.<ref name=\"rauf\"/> By November 2014, he was recovering, could walk short distances without assistance, and was re-learning to write and play guitar, according to Davis.<ref>{{Cite web |url=http://tasteofcountry.com/randy-travis-health-recovery-update/ |title=Randy Travis' Fiancee Updates His Recovery |last=Whitaker |first=Sterling |website=Tasteofcountry.com |date=November 7, 2014 |access-date=November 8, 2014}}</ref>\n\nWhile the stroke removed most of Travis's ability to sing, he has made sporadic onstage appearances to perform in limited capacity. In 2015, Travis made a guest appearance at the Academy of Country Music awards ceremony, one of his first public appearances after his stroke, where [[Lee Brice]] paid tribute to him by singing \"Forever and Ever, Amen\".<ref name=\"secretly\">{{cite web | url=https://abcnews.go.com/Entertainment/randy-travis-secretly-marries-mary-davis/story?id=30472284 | title=Randy Travis Secretly Marries Mary Davis | publisher=ABC News | date=April 21, 2015 | accessdate=January 20, 2024}}</ref> Travis sang \"Amazing Grace\" in March 2016 at an induction ceremony into the Country Music Hall of Fame, where Davis accompanied him.<ref name=\"damaged\"/><ref name=\"mary\"/> Despite this, Travis's speech remained limited when he was interviewed by ''The Tennessean'' in 2017, and he remained largely reliant on a wheelchair.<ref name=\"damaged\"/> He appeared onstage with singer [[Michael Ray (singer)|Michael Ray]] during a cover performance of \"Forever and Ever, Amen\" in June 2017, to which Travis contributed the final \"Amen.\"<ref>{{Cite news |url=http://people.com/country/randy-travis-cma-fest-2017-michael-ray/ |title=Without Words, Randy Travis Makes Fans' Dreams Come True |last=Kruh |first=Nancy |date=June 2017 |work=People Country |access-date=January 26, 2019}}</ref> He did the same during his 60th birthday party, hosted by the Grand Ole Opry on May 4, 2019.<ref>{{Cite web |url=https://tasteofcountry.com/randy-travis-60th-grand-ole-opry/ |title=Randy Travis Celebrates 60th Birthday at Grand Ole Opry |last=Hermanson |first=Wendy |website=Tasteofcountry.com |date=May 5, 2019 |access-date=September 29, 2019}}</ref>\n\n==Awards==\n{{Main|List of awards and nominations received by Randy Travis}}\n\nTravis has won seven Grammy Awards, six CMA Awards, and eleven ACM awards.<ref name=\"grammy\"/><ref name=\"acm\"/><ref name=\"cma\"/>\n\n==Discography==\n{{Main|Randy Travis albums discography|Randy Travis singles discography}}\n;Studio albums\n{{Div col}}\n*''[[Storms of Life]]'' (1986)\n*''[[Always & Forever (Randy Travis album)|Always & Forever]]'' (1987)\n*''[[Old 8\u00d710]]'' (1988)\n*''[[No Holdin' Back]]'' (1989) \n*''[[An Old Time Christmas]]'' (1989)\n*''[[Heroes & Friends]]'' (1990)\n*''[[High Lonesome (Randy Travis album)|High Lonesome]]'' (1991)\n*''[[Wind in the Wire]]'' (1993)\n*''[[This Is Me (Randy Travis album)|This Is |publisher=St. Martin's Press |isbn=0-312-04412-7 |url=https://archive.org/details/randytraviskingo00cusi}}\n* {{cite book|last1=Travis|first1=Randy|last2=Abraham|first2=Ken|title=Forever and Ever, publisher=Billboard Books |last=Roland |first=Tom | year=1991 | isbn=0-8230-7553-2}}\n* {{cite book|last=Whitburn|first=Joel|authorlink=Joel Whitburn|title=Hot Country Songs 1944 to 2017|publisher=Record Research, Inc|year=2017|isbn=978-0-89820-229-8}}\n\n== External links ==\n{{Commonscat}}\n*{{allmusic}}\n* {{IMDb name|0871430|Randy Travis}}\n\n{{Randy Travis}}\n{{Grand Ole singer-songwriter (1918\u20132009)}}\n{{good article}}\n{{Use mdy dates|date=August 2020}}\n{{Infobox person\n| name               = Hank Locklin\n| image              = Hank Locklin--1966.jpg\n| alt                = Hank Locklin, 1966.\n| caption            = Locklin in 1966\n| birth_name         = Lawrence Hankins Locklin\n| birth_date         = {{birth date|1918|2|15|}}<ref    = 6\n| module             = {{Infobox musical artist\n| embed              = yes\n| background         = solo_singer\n| genre              = {{hlist|[[Country music|Country]]|[[Gospel {{URL|http://hanklocklin.com/}}\n}}\n}}\n\n'''Lawrence Hankins Locklin'''<ref name=\"Encyclopedia\"/> (February 15, 1918 \u2013 March 8, 2009) was an American [[country music]] singer-songwriter. He had 70 chart singles, including two number one hits on ''[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]]''{{'}}s country chart. His biggest hits included \"[[Send Me the Pillow You Dream On]]\" and his signature \"[[Please Help Me, I'm Falling]]\".<ref name=pc10>{{Gilliland |url=https://digital.library.unt.edu/ark:/67531/metadc19758/m1/ |title=Show 10 \u2013 Tennessee Firebird: American country music before and after Elvis. [Part 2] }}</ref> The latter also went to number eight on the [[Billboard Hot 100|''Billboard'' Hot 100]] pop music chart. ''Billboard''{{'}}s 100th anniversary issue listed it as the second most successful country single of the [[rock and roll]] era. It sold over one million copies, and was awarded a [[music recording sales certification|gold disc]] by the [[Recording Industry Association of America|RIAA]].<ref name=\"Golden Discs\">{{cite book |last1=Murrells |first1=Joseph |title=The Book of Golden Discs |date=1978 |publisher=Barrie and Jenkins Ltd |location=London |isbn=0-214-20512-6 |edition=2nd |url=https://archive.org/details/bookofgoldendisc00murr/page/126}}</ref>\n\nLocklin was born and raised in Florida. He developed a fondness for country music following an accident in his childhood. He learned to play the guitar during his recovery and began performing locally as well. In his early adulthood, he formed his own band called the Rocky Mountain Playboys, which played gigs and performed on local radio. Locklin was heard singing during one of these gigs, which led to his first recording contract in 1949. He had his first major hit on the ''Billboard'' country chart the same year. His 1953 single \"[[Let Me Be the One (Hank Locklin song)|Let Me Be the One]]\" was his first to top the country chart.\n\nIn 1955, Locklin signed with [[RCA Records|RCA Victor Records]] and under the production of [[Chet Atkins]], he had his biggest success as a country artist. He had a string of major hits during the late 1950s and early 1960s. He also joined the cast of the [[Grand Ole Opry]] and remained a member for nearly 50 years. He became a major concert attraction internationally as well, touring overseas in Europe beginning in the 1960s and 70s. Locklin also recorded a handful of concept albums during this period, which made him among the genre's first artists to release albums centered around a theme. He later recorded for other labels, including [[MGM Records|MGM]], [[Plantation Records|Plantation]] and Coldwater. Despite his country success, Locklin never lived in Nashville, choosing to stay in Florida. Locklin died in 2009 at the age of 91.\n\n==Early life==\nLocklin was born and raised in [[McLellan, Florida]]. He was the youngest of four children born to a musically inclined family. Although he had a fondness for music in his early years, he became more interested following an accident.<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\">{{cite web |last1=Erlewine |first1=Michael |title=Hank Locklin: Biography & History |url=https://www.allmusic.com/artist/hank-locklin-mn0000666540/biography |website=[[Allmusic]] |access-date=30 November 2020}}</ref> At age 9, he was hit by a school bus. \"It almost mashed me flatter than a fritter,\" he recalled.<ref name=\"NY Times\">{{cite news |last1=Martin |first1=Douglas |title=Hank Locklin, Country Singer, Is Dead at 91 |work=The New York Times |date=March 10, 2009 |url=https://www.nytimes.com/2009/03/10/arts/music/10locklin.html |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210210214105/https://www.nytimes.com/2009/03/10/arts/music/10locklin.html |access-date=30 November 2020|archive-date=February 10, 2021 }}</ref> He learned to play the guitar during his recovery and continued pursuing music through his teenage years.<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/> He also won several talent contests and performing at a radio station in Pensacola, Florida. To pursue his music full-time, Locklin dropped out of high school. However, working as a musician amounted to a small income. In one instance, he recalled being paid $2 for working a gig at a Florida roadhouse.<ref name=\"Encyclopedia\"/> To make ends meet, he worked odd jobs, including working on a farm and a shipyard.<ref name=\"NY Times\"/>\n\nDuring the early 1940s, Locklin was dismissed from participating in World War II due to the bus accident in his childhood (which still affected his leg). Instead, he continued performing. Locklin performed in nearby states, including regular gigs with bands in [[Mobile, Alabama]]. He also began composing his own material, largely influenced by the music of [[Ernest Tubb]]. He then joined the band of country musician [[Jimmy Swan]] and made regular concert appearances. Locklin formed his own backing band in 1947 named the Rocky Mountain Playboys. The group performed regularly on radio stations. Their sponsor was businessman Elmer Laird, who intended to form a record label to get Locklin's music recorded. However, he was killed in a stabbing incident shortly after his proposal.<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/> Instead, Locklin's association with producer [[Pappy Daily]] helped him sign with [[Four Star Records]].<ref name=\"Country Standard Time\">{{cite web |last1=Burke |first1=Ken |title=Hungry Hank Locklin returns with \"Generations in Song\" |url=https://www.countrystandardtime.com/d/article.asp?xid=573 |website=Country Standard Time |access-date=30 November 2020}}</ref><ref name=\"CMT\">{{cite web |title=Grand Ole Opry Member Hank Locklin Dies at Age 91 |url=http://www.cmt.com/news/1606538/grand-ole-opry-member-hank-locklin-dies-at-age-91/ |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210210215111/http://www.cmt.com/news/1606538/grand-ole-opry-member-hank-locklin-dies-at-age-91/ |url-status=dead |archive-date=February 10, 2021 |website=[[Country Music Television]] |access-date=30 November 2020}}</ref>\n\n==Career==\n===1949\u20131954: Early success===\nLocklin secured a contract with Four Star Records through his association with Pappy Daily. Daily brought him to the attention of Bill McCall of Four Star where he signed his first recording contract in 1949.<ref name=\"Country Standard Time\"/> Also in 1949, Locklin joined the cast of the ''[[Louisiana Hayride]]'' in Shreveport, Louisiana.<ref name=\"Independent\">{{cite web |last1=Leigh |first1=Spencer |title=Hank Locklin: Country singer and guitarist |url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/obituaries/hank-locklin-country-singer-and-guitarist-1687238.html |website=[[The Independent]] |date=May 18, 2009 |access-date=1 December 2020}}</ref> He had his first major hit in 1949 with a self-penned single titled \"[[The Same Sweet Girl]]\".<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/> The song reached the top 10 of the ''[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]]'' country singles chart, peaking at number eight.<ref name=\"Billboard Singles\">{{cite book|last=Whitburn|first=Joel|title=Hot Country Songs 1944 to 2008|publisher=Record Research, Inc.|year=2008|isbn=978-0-89820-177-2}}</ref> Despite having a hit, Locklin did not see many royalties from its success. Because his recording contract allowed for McCall to gain all of the publishing rights, McCall also made all the money from its success. \"I never made no money with him. My understanding was that he liked to go to Vegas. I guess Bill was just a guy who liked to take everything,\" Locklin recalled in 2001.<ref name=\"Country Standard Time\"/>\n\nLocklin's follow-up singles did not become hits, which caused further financial difficulties.<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/> He even recorded the original version of his future hit \"Send Me the Pillow You Dream On\". Although it was not a hit, Locklin did recall the song getting a warm reception after he performed it live on a radio show.<ref name=\"Country Standard Time\"/> By this time, McCall had arranged for Locklin's material to be leased to [[Decca Records]], which was headed by [[Owen Bradley]]. With this partnership, he had his second major hit in 1953 titled \"[[Let Me Be the One (Hank Locklin song)|Let Me Be the One]]\".<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/> The single became his first to reach the number one position on the ''Billboard'' country chart. \"Let Me Be the One\" spent a total of three weeks at the country chart summit. His follow-up Four Star releases were unsuccessful.<ref name=\"Billboard Singles\"/> Furthermore, Locklin still was receiving little royalties from his success with the label. In order for McCall to not claim them as his own, he started composing songs under his wife's name. Cuts such as \"These Ruins Belong to You\" were written under the pen name Willa. After an argument with McCall, he left Four Star Records.<ref name=\"Independent\"/>\n\n===1955\u20131974: Move to RCA Records and biggest success===\nIn 1955, Locklin switched to the larger [[RCA Records]]. Collaborating with producer [[Chet Atkins]], Locklin developed a simpler musical style. His recordings often included heavy [[acoustic guitar]] and [[piano]] production.<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/> These styles were individualized by that of [[The Nashville A-Team]], a group of studio musicians that included [[Floyd Cramer]] and several others.<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/><ref name=\"Independent\"/> In 1956, he had his first hit with RCA titled \"[[Why Baby Why]]\". The single reached the top 10 of the ''Billboard'' [[Hot Country Songs|Country & Western Records Most Played By Folk Disk Jockeys]] chart, becoming his first major hit in three years.<ref name=\"Billboard Singles\"/> It was followed in 1957 by \"[[Geisha Girl (song)|Geisha Girl]]\".<ref name=\"Independent\"/> The song reached number five on the ''Billboard'' country chart,<ref name=\"Billboard Singles\"/> and was his first to reach the [[Billboard Hot 100|pop chart]], climbing to number 66.<ref name=\"Pop Singles\">{{cite book |last1=Whitburn |first1=Joel |title=Joel Whitburn's Billboard Top Pop Singles 1955-2006 |date=2008 |publisher=Record Research |isbn=978-0898201727}}</ref> The hit was included on his debut studio album ''[[Foreign Love]].'' Marketed toward overseas servicemen, the record was one of several [[concept album]]s released by Locklin during his career.<ref name=\"Independent\"/> In 1958, Locklin had major hits with \"[[It's a Little More Like Heaven]]\" and a remake of \"Send Me the Pillow You Dream On\".<ref name=\"Billboard Singles\"/>\n\nLocklin's singles over the next two years were unsuccessful.<ref name=\"Billboard Singles\"/> In 1960, he was brought to the attention of a song composed by Hal Blair and [[Don Robertson (songwriter)|Don Robertson]]. Both writers had the idea of writing a \"cheating song\" that centered around a man's conscience. Blair brought in elements from his own marital troubles to write the song. Their finished product was the tune \"[[Please Help Me, I'm Falling]]\". The song was first offered to [[Jim Reeves]], who turned it down. Locklin believed it to be a hit and decided to cut it in the studio.<ref name=\"Independent\"/> Released as a single in 1960, \"Please Help Me, I'm Falling\" reached number one on the ''Billboard'' country songs chart in May.<ref>{{cite magazine |title=\"Please Help Me, I'm Falling\" (Country Songs) |url=https://www.billboard.com/artist/hank-locklin/chart-history/csi/ |magazine=[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]] |access-date=1 December 2020}}</ref> It spent a total of 14 weeks at the top of country charts.<ref name=\"NY Times\"/> It also became Locklin's biggest hit on the ''Billboard'' pop chart, reaching the number eight position in August 1960.<ref>{{cite magazine |title=\"Please Help Me, I'm Falling\" chart history |url=https://www.billboard.com/artist/hank-locklin/chart-history/hsi/ |magazine=[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]] |access-date=1 December 2020}}</ref> In the United Kingdom, the song reached the top 10 of the national charts as well.<ref name=\"UK Singles\">{{cite book|title=Guinness World Records British Hit Singles 15th Edition|year=2002|page=301|publisher=[[Guinness World Records]] Limited|location= London, UK|isbn=0-85112-187-X}}</ref> \"Please Help Me, I'm Falling\" became the biggest hit of Locklin's career and his biggest-selling single.<ref name=\"Billboard Singles\"/> The song's success led to an invitation to join the [[Grand Ole Opry]], which he accepted in 1960. He remained with the program for 49 years.<ref name=\"Irish Times\">{{cite news |title=Hank Locklin |url=https://www.irishtimes.com/news/hank-locklin-1.748471 |newspaper=[[The Irish Times]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref><ref>{{cite web |last1=Darden |first1=Beville |title=Grand Ole Opry Star Hank Locklin Dies at 91 |url=https://theboot.com/grand-ole-opry-star-hank-locklin-dies-at-91/ |website=The Boot |date=March 9, 2009 |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref>\n{{listen|filename=Hank Locklin--Please Help Me I'm Falling.ogg |title=\"Please Help Me, I'm Falling\"|description=In 1960, \"Please Help Me, I'm Falling\" was released on RCA Victor and became Locklin's best-known hit.|format=[[Ogg]]||pos=left}}\n\nLocklin did not reach the top of the country charts again, but he continued having commercial success with RCA Victor. In 1961, he had three top 20 hits with \"[[One Step Ahead of My Past]]\", \"[[From Here to There to You]]\" and \"[[You're the Reason]]\".<ref name=\"Billboard Singles\"/> In November 1961, the single \"[[Happy Birthday to Me (Hank Locklin song)|Happy Birthday to Me]]\" (written by [[Bill Anderson (singer)|Bill Anderson]]) reached number seven on the ''Billboard'' country songs chart.<ref>{{cite magazine |title=\"Happy Birthday to Me\" chart history |url=https://www.billboard.com/artist/hank-locklin/chart-history/csi/ |magazine=[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref> It was followed by \"[[Happy Journey (song)|Happy Journey]]\", which went to number 10 on the country singles list in February 1962.<ref>{{cite magazine |title=\"Happy Journey\" chart history |url=https://www.billboard.com/artist/hank-locklin/chart-history/csi/ |magazine=[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref> An album of the [[Happy Journey (album)|same name]] followed in 1962, and it included the top 20 single \"[[We're Gonna Go Fishin']]\".<ref>{{cite web |title=Hank Locklin -- ''Happy Journey'' (1962, LP) |url=https://www.discogs.com/Hank-Locklin-Happy-Journey/release/6191352 |website=[[Discogs]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref><ref>{{cite magazine |title=\"We're Gonna Go Fishin'\" chart history |url=https://www.billboard.com/artist/hank-locklin/chart-history/csi/ |magazine=[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref> The song also became a major hit in the United Kingdom.<ref name=\"UK Singles\"/> His 1964 song \"[[Followed Closely by My Teardrops]]\" was his only major hit for several years.<ref>{{cite magazine |title=\"Followed Closely by My Teardrops\" chart history |url=https://www.billboard.com/artist/hank-locklin/chart-history/csi/ |magazine=[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref><ref name=\"Billboard Singles\"/>\n\nLocklin expanded his recording of concept albums during the 1960s. This began with a tribute effort to [[Roy Acuff]] titled ''[[A Tribute to Roy Acuff: The King of Country Music]].'' It featured a collection of Acuff's best-known songs, including \"[[Wabash Cannonball]]\" and \"Once More\".<ref>{{cite web |title=Hank Locklin -- ''A Trubute to Roy Acuff: The King of Country Music'' (1962, LP) |url=https://www.discogs.com/Hank-Locklin-A-Tribute-To-Roy-Acuff-King-Of-Country-Music/master/352009 |website=[[Discogs]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref> The album was reviewed positively by ''Billboard'' in 1962. Writers noted their appreciation for the fact that Locklin chose to sing Acuff's songs in his own style rather than emulate him.<ref>{{cite magazine |title=Spotlight Albums of the Week: Album Reviews |magazine=[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]] |date=October 20, 1962 |volume=74 |issue=42 |page=32 |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=5xcEAAAAMBAJ&q=hank+locklin+irish+songs+country+style+billboard&pg=PA32}}</ref> In later years, [[Allmusic]] rated the album three out of five stars.<ref>{{cite web |title=''A Tribute to Roy Acuff, King of Country Music'': Hank Locklin: Songs, Reviews, Credits |url=https://www.allmusic.com/album/a-tribute-to-roy-acuff-king-of-country-music-mw0000892522 |website=[[Allmusic]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref> With an expanding fan base in Ireland, Locklin recorded 1963's ''[[Irish Songs, Country Style]].''<ref name=\"Irish Times\"/> The following year, he recorded a [[Hank Locklin Sings Hank Williams|studio album]] of [[Hank Williams]]' songs. Allmusic's Greg Adams  rated it 2.5 out of 5 stars, calling Locklin \"a good yodeler [who] seems to struggle a little.\"<ref>{{cite web |last1=Adams |first1=Greg |title=''Sings Hank Williams'': Hank Locklin: Songs, Reviews, Credits |url=https://www.allmusic.com/album/sings-hank-williams-mw0000890552 |website=[[Allmusic]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref> Meanwhile, 1965's ''[[My Kind of Country Music]]'' fared better with critics. Reviewer Thom Owens gave the release 4.5 stars and praised his \"gift for tear-jerking ballads and pure traditional country.\"<ref>{{cite web |title=''My Kind of Country Music'': Hank Locklin: Songs, Reviews, Credits |url=https://www.allmusic.com/album/my-kind-of-country-music-mw0001883500 |website=[[Allmusic]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref> Locklin's 1966 studio release ''[[The Girls Get Prettier]]'' was his first to chart the ''Billboard'' [[Top Country Albums]] list, reaching number 26.<ref>{{cite magazine |title=''The Girls Get Prettier'' chart history |url=https://www.billboard.com/artist/hank-locklin/chart-history/clp/ |magazine=[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref>\n\nIn 1968, Locklin returned with his first top 10 hit in several years titled \"[[The Country Hall of Fame (song)|The Country Hall of Fame]]\".<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/> Its corresponding studio effort of the same name was also successful, reaching number 20 on the country albums chart.<ref>{{cite magazine |title=''Country Hall of Fame'' album chart history |url=https://www.billboard.com/artist/hank-locklin/chart-history/clp/ |magazine=[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref> Allmusic gave it a warm response in its review, calling it \"one of his best albums of the late '60s.\"<ref>{{cite web |last1=Owens |first1=Thom |title=''Country Hall of Fame'': Songs, Reviews, Credits |url=https://www.allmusic.com/album/country-hall-of-fame-mw0000889297 |website=[[Allmusic]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref> Following its release, Locklin saw declining radio success on the American country charts. He had his last top 40 single on the ''Billboard'' country songs survey was 1969's \"[[Where the Blue of the Night (Meets the Gold of the Day)|Where the Blue of the Night Meets the Gold of the Day]]\".<ref name=\"Billboard Singles\"/> At the start of the early 1970s, he spent time touring and performing in Europe, where he became popular.<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/> He was among country music's early artists to perform at military bases and clubs in Europe.<ref name=\"Independent\"/> He also toured with producer Chet Atkins in Japan during the early 1970s.<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/> Locklin remained with RCA Victor until 1974.<ref name=\"Billboard Singles\"/> Among the highlights in his remaining RCA years was a [[Hank Locklin & Danny Davis & the Nashville Brass|collaborative record]] with [[Danny Davis (country musician)|Danny Davis]] and the Nashville Brass in 1970. It featured re-recordings of some his bigger hits.<ref>{{cite web |title=''Hank Locklin & Danny Davis & the Nashville Brass'' (1970) |url=https://www.discogs.com/Hank-Locklin-And-Danny-Davis-The-Nashville-Brass-Hank-Locklin-Danny-Davis-The-Nashville-Brass/master/834997 |website=[[Discogs]] |access-date=9 December 2020}}</ref> His final album for the label was 1972's ''[[The Mayor of McLellan, Florida]].''<ref>{{cite web |title=Hank Locklin -- ''Mayor of McLellan, Florida'' (1972, LP) |url=https://www.discogs.com/Hank-Locklin-The-Mayor-Of-McLellan-Florida/release/7587150 |website=[[Discogs]] |access-date=2 December 2020}}</ref>\n[[File:Hank Locklin.png|thumb|right|200px|Locklin in a trade ad for ''[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]]'' magazine, 1968]]\n\n===1975\u20132007: Later years===\nFollowing his departure from RCA Victor Records, Locklin recorded for various other labels, beginning with [[MGM Records]].<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/> In 1975, his [[Hank Locklin (1975 album)|self-titled studio album]] was released on the label and included a collection of 10 tracks. The record was produced by fellow country artist [[Mel Tillis]].<ref>{{cite web |title=Hank Locklin -- ''Hank Locklin'' (1975, LP) |url=https://www.discogs.com/Hank-Locklin-Hank-Locklin/release/7001292 |website=[[Discogs]] |access-date=3 December 2020}}</ref> Three singles were spawned from the album, but failed to become commercially successful.<ref name=\"Billboard Singles\"/> He then moved to [[Plantation Records]] in 1977 where he recorded the studio record ''[[There Never Was a Time]]''. It was produced by [[Shelby Singleton]] and featured 10 tracks of original material.<ref>{{cite web |title=Hank Locklin -- ''There Never Was a Time'' (1977, LP) |url=https://www.discogs.com/Hank-Locklin-There-Never-Was-A-Time/release/7001082 |website=[[Discogs]] |access-date=3 December 2020}}</ref> Four singles were spawned from the album, which were unsuccessful.<ref name=\"Billboard Singles\"/> He also turned his attention to other opportunities during this period. In the 1970s, Locklin hosted a handful of television programs in Dallas, Texas and Houston, Texas. He also continued performing overseas, particularly in Ireland. He also remained an active performer on the Grand Ole Opry.<ref name=\"Irish Times\"/><ref name=\"Encyclopedia\"/> During this period, he was made the honorary mayor in his hometown of [[McLellan, Florida]].<ref name=\"Hank Locklin\">{{cite web |title=Hank Locklin Biography: 10/2006 |url=http://www.hanklocklin.com/history.html |website=Hank Locklin.com |access-date=3 December 2020}}</ref> The honor was given to him after he was nicknamed the \"Mayor of McLellan\" by radio and television host [[Ralph Emery]].<ref>{{cite web |title=Hank Locklin |url=https://www.brewtonstandard.com/2009/03/11/hank-locklinbrewton/ |website=Brewton Standard |date=March 11, 2009 |access-date=3 December 2020}}</ref>\n\nLocklin's career began slowing after the 1970s. In the early 2000s, his son (Hank Adam Locklin) encouraged his father to return to the recording studio. In 2001, Locklin's first album in decades was released, titled ''[[Generations in Song]].'' The project was produced by his son and featured session instrumentation from some of the original Nashville A-Team. It also featured collaborations with [[Vince Gill]], [[Dolly Parton]] and others.<ref name=\"Country Standard Time\"/><ref>{{cite web |title=Hank Locklin -- ''Generations in Song'' (Coldwater Records) (2001, CD) |url=https://www.discogs.com/Hank-Locklin-Generations-In-Song/release/13248178 |website=[[Discogs]] |access-date=3 December 2020}}</ref> The album received positive reviews from writers and critics following its release. Bruce Eder of Allmusic gave it four stars and praised Locklin's voice: \"His voice sounds at least three decades fresher than it has a right to, and the harmonizing and the playing are first-rate, along with the arrangements, which makes this more than an exercise in nostalgia.\"<ref>{{cite web |last1=Eder |first1=Bruce |title=''Generations in Song'': Hank Locklin: Songs, Reviews, Credits |url=https://www.allmusic.com/album/generations-in-song-mw0000330634 |website=[[Allmusic]] |access-date=3 December 2020}}</ref> ''[[No Depression (magazine)|No Depression]]'' also gave the album a warm response, calling its production to have \"clean arrangements\" that allow his \"clear voice to shine through.\"<ref>{{cite web |title=Hank Locklin \u2013 ''Generations in Song'' |url=https://www.nodepression.com/album-reviews/hank-locklin-generations-in-song/ |website=[[No Depression (magazine)|No Depression]] |access-date=3 December 2020}}</ref> Ken Burke of ''Country Standard Time'' also gave the album a positive review. \"The Florida-born tenor can boast not only that he clawed his way out of dire poverty, but built a musical legacy that he can share with his family,\" Burke wrote.<ref name=\"Country Standard Time\"/>\n\nIn 2006, Locklin released his final album titled ''[[By the Grace of God: The Gospel Album]]''. The album was a collection of [[Gospel music|gospel]] songs that featured collaborations with [[The Oak Ridge Boys]] and [[The Jordanaires]].<ref name=\"Irish Times\"/><ref>{{cite web |title=''By the Grace of God: The Gospel Album'': Hank Locklin: Songs, Reviews, Credits |url=https://www.allmusic.com/album/by-the-grace-of-god-the-gospel-album-mw0000563297 |website=[[Allmusic]] |access-date=3 December 2020}}</ref> The record was issued on Yell Records. It was reviewed favorably by Larry Stephens of ''Country Standard Time'', who praised Locklin's youthful voice and songwriting effort.<ref>{{cite web |last1=Stephens |first1=Larry |title=Hank Locklin -- ''By the Grace of God'' |url=https://www.countrystandardtime.com/d/cdreview.asp?xid=3433 |website=Country Standard Time |access-date=3 December 2020}}</ref> Also in 2006, Locklin appeared on ''Country Pop Legends'' in which he performed \"Send Me the Pillow That You Dream On\", and \"Please Help Me I'm Falling\".<ref>{{cite web |title=Country Pop Legends (My Music) |url=https://azpbs.org/pressroom/2011/08/country-pop-legends-my-music/ |website=[[PBS|Arizona PBS]] |access-date=13 March 2021}}</ref> In his final years of performing, Locklin continued appearing at the Grand Ole Opry. In September 2007, he made his last appearance at the venue.<ref>{{cite web |title=Country singer Hank Locklin dies |url=https://variety.com/2009/music/news/country-singer-hank-locklin-dies-1118000984/ |website=[[Variety (magazine)|Variety]] |date=March 9, 2009 |access-date=3 December 2020}}</ref>\n\n==Personal life and death==\nLocklin was married twice. His first marriage to Willa Jean Murphy began in 1938, but ended in divorce.<ref name=\"Independent\"/> In 1970, he married Anita Crooks of Brewton, Alabama. He had two sons and four daughters, 12 grandchildren, and eight great-grandchildren.<ref name=\"NY Times\"/> Among his children is his son Hank Adam Locklin, a producer and musician. Locklin's son helped manage his career and produce some of his later records.<ref name=\"Country Standard Time\"/> In the 1960s, Locklin built a ranch house called The Singing L, located in McClellan.<ref name=\"AP\">{{cite news|url=http://www.volunteertv.com/news/headlines/40960202.html|title=Grand Ole Opry member Hank Locklin dies in Alabama|date=March 9, 2009|publisher=Associated Press via [[WVLT-TV]]|access-date=March 9, 2009|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20090313040801/http://www.volunteertv.com/news/headlines/40960202.html|archive-date=March 13, 2009}}</ref>\n\nHe moved to Brewton, where he remained throughout his later years, and died there at home in the early morning on March 8, 2009.<ref name=\"AP\"/> He is buried in the United Methodist Church Mount Carmel Cemetery, located in [[Jay, Florida]]. Following his death, longtime friend Sandy Wyatt stated \"Not only was (Hank) a great artist, he was the nicest man you\u2019d ever meet.\"<ref name=\"Pensacola\">{{cite web |last1=Kennedy |first1=Emma |title=Santa Rosa County country superstar's story to air on WSRE |url=https://www.pnj.com/story/entertainment/2020/11/11/hank-locklin-santa-rosa-county-country-superstar-story-air-wsre/6226604002/ |website=[[Pensacola News Journal]] |access-date=4 December 2020}}</ref>\n\n==Musical styles==\nLocklin's musical style stems from country music's [[honky-tonk]] and Nashville Sound sub-genres.<ref name=\"NY Times\"/><ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/> In his early years, Locklin was primarily a honky-tonk singer that also incorporated elements of [[Texas country music|Texas shuffles]]. His early records on the Four Star label embodied this particular style. His early songs also took influence from other artists, including [[Hank Williams]]. In reviewing Locklin's 1997 compilation, Bruce Eder wrote that songs such as \"Born to Ramble\" drew a strong influence from Williams.<ref name=\"Eder\">{{cite web |last1=Eder |first1=Bruce |title=''Send Me the Pillow That You Dream On'': Hank Williams: Songs, Reviews, Credits |url=https://www.allmusic.com/album/send-me-the-pillow-you-dream-on-box-set-mw0000604808 |website=[[Allmusic]] |access-date=4 December 2020}}</ref> Locklin also emulated the musical styles of Ernest Tubb (among the artists responsible for the formation of country's honky-tonk style).<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/>\n\nLocklin's sound and style became individualized once he signed with RCA Records in 1955.<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/> His producer Chet Atkins helped create his image by incorporating a softer Nashville Sound instrumentation. Writer Greg Adams described Locklin's RCA years as having \"sweet vocal choruses\" backed by horn and string sections.<ref>{{cite web |last1=Adams |first1=Greg |title=''Please Help Me I'm Falling'': Hank Locklin: Songs, Reviews, Credits |url=https://www.allmusic.com/album/please-help-me-im-falling-box-set-mw0000645770 |website=[[Allmusic]] |access-date=4 December 2020}}</ref> In a 2001 interview, Locklin recounted how Chet Atkins created musical arrangements by using the guitar he kept in his office: \"Then, all of a sudden he'd pick it up and hit a chord or two on something or other that floated through his head. He was so good, and he really helped me a lot with RCA.\"<ref name=\"Country Standard Time\"/>\n\n==Legacy==\nLocklin is considered by many writers to be one of country music's \"great tenors.\"<ref name=\"Allmusic Bio\"/><ref>{{cite web |title=Hank Locklin Obituary (2009) |url=https://obits.tennessean.com/obituaries/tennessean/obituary.aspx?n=hank-locklin&pid=125010822 |website=[[The Tennessean|Tennessean]] |access-date=4 December 2020}}</ref><ref name=\"NY Times\"/> Critics have noted that his distinctive high-pitch singing made him standout from other performers of the era. In a review of the compilation ''RCA Country Legends,'' [[Stephen Thomas Erlewine]] highlighted this characteristic: \"Locklin held firm to his country ideals, and whittled down to the essentials as they are here, they offer proof of why country connoisseurs consider him among the best pure singers in the genre.\"<ref>{{cite web |last1=Erlewine |first1=Stephen |title=''RCA Country Legends'': Hank Locklin: Songs, Reviews, Credits |url=https://www.allmusic.com/album/rca-country-legends-mw0000021785 |website=[[Allmusic]] |access-date=4 December 2020}}</ref> Locklin also helped define the [[Nashville Sound]] sub-genre of country music.<ref name=\"NY Times\"/> This particular style brought a cosmopolitan feel and a smooth quality to country music. \"I\u2019ve been blessed to have hit songs that are timeless,\" Locklin commented in 2001.<ref>{{cite web |title=Country music star Hank Locklin, 91, dies |url=https://oklahoman.com/article/3351850/country-music-star-hank-locklin-91-dies |website=[[The Oklahoman]] |date=March 10, 2009 |access-date=4 December 2020}}</ref>\n\nLocklin's legacy can be linked with his 49-year membership to the Grand Ole Opry. He was known for his \"folksy\" style of humor and often joked with his Opry audience by saying \"We\u2019ll treat you so many ways you\u2019re bound to like one of \u2019em.\"<ref name=\"NY Times\"/> In 2007 he was inducted to the [[Florida Artists Hall of Fame]].<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.florida-arts.org/programs/ahf/displayArtist.cfm?member=40 |title=Lawrence Hankins \"Hank \" Locklin \u2013 Division of Cultural Affairs \u2013 Florida Department of State |publisher=Florida-arts.org |access-date=August 18, 2015}}</ref> In November 2020, PBS aired a documentary on Locklin's career and legacy titled ''Hank Locklin: Country Music's Timeless Tenor.'' Fellow country artists spoke of his influence on their careers, including [[Dolly Parton]] and [[Dwight Yoakam]].<ref name=\"Pensacola\"/>\n\n==Discography==\n{{main|Hank Locklin This format is deprecated, and in the future the new format will always be used."}},"query":{"normalized":[{"from":"Barbara_Fairchild","to":"Barbara Fairchild"}],"pages":{"5223441":{"pageid":5223441,"ns":0,"title":"Barbara Fairchild","revisions":[{"contentformat":"text/x-wiki","contentmodel":"wikitext","*":"{{short description|American country and gospel singer|bot=PearBOT 5}}\n{{about||the American food journalist|Barbara Fairchild (journalist)}}\n{{Infobox musical artist\n| image           = BarbaraFairchildAtHerDiner.jpg\n| name            = Barbara Fairchild\n| image_size      = <!-- Only for images smaller than 220 pixels -->\n| birth_name      = Barbara Fairchild\n| birth_date      = {{birth date and age|1950|11|12}}  \n| birth_place     = [[Knobel, Arkansas|Knobel]], Arkansas, United States\n| genre           = [[Country music|Country]], [[Gospel Music|gospel]]\n| occupation      = [[Singing|Singer]], [[songwriter]]\n| years_active    = 1969&ndash;present\n| label           = [[Columbia Records]]<br/>Daywind Records\n| associated_acts = [[Sharon White (singer)|Sharon White]], [[Billy Walker (musician)|Billy Walker]], [[Connie Smith]]\n| website         = [http://www.barbarafairchild.com/site/ Official website]\n}}\n\n'''Barbara Fairchild''' (born November 12, 1950)<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\">{{cite book|title=[[Encyclopedia of Popular Music|The Guinness Who's Who of Country Music]]|editor=[[Colin Larkin (writer)|Colin Larkin]]|publisher=[[Guinness Publishing]]|date=1993|edition=First|isbn=0-85112-726-6|page=136}}</ref> is an American [[country music|country]] and [[gospel music|gospel]] singer, who is best known for her hit 1973 country song \"[[Teddy Bear Song]]\" and other country hits.<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/>\n\n==Biography==\n===Early life and beginnings in Nashville===\nShe was born in [[Knobel, Arkansas]], United States.<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> Fairchild started her career at a young age singing country music. She cut her first single at 15 years old. In 1963, she moved to [[St. Louis]], [[Missouri]], and by 1965, she was a regular on a local TV show and recorded for a local label, Norman Records,<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> but none of her singles released were much more than regional hits.<ref name=music>{{cite web|last=Loftus|first=Johnny|title=Artist Biography by Johnny Loftus|url=http://www.allmusic.com/artist/barbara-fairchild-mn0000125352/biography|website=[[AllMusic]]|access-date=19 October 2013}}</ref>\n\nIn 1968, after high school graduation, she decided to follow her dream and moved to [[Nashville, Tennessee|Nashville]].<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> She briefly signed with [[Kapp Records]] with no success.<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> She also recorded briefly for [[MCA Records]]. After this, she met producer [[Billy Sherrill]], who had discovered [[Tammy Wynette]]. He listened to Fairchild's songs and decided that she was ready for a major record deal, and he signed her with [[Columbia Records]] in 1969.<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> Her first single in 1969, \"Love Is A Gentle Thing\", was a minor hit as was her next single, \"A Woman's Hand\".<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> In 1970, she scored her first Top 40 hit with \"A Girl Who'll Satisfy Her Man\". Between 1970 and 1972, Fairchild scored four more Top 40 hits, the biggest of these being \"Love's Old Song\" and \"Thanks For The Mem'ries\".<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/>\n\n===The success of \"Teddy Bear Song\"===\n1973 would turn out to be the breakthrough year for Fairchild as she released the biggest hit of her career with \"Teddy Bear Song\".<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> It became a number 1 hit on the country charts, and reached the pop charts as well, peaking at No.&nbsp;32.<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> It was nominated for a [[Grammy Award]] that year. \"Teddy Bear Song\" spent two weeks at the No. 1 spot. She followed up \"Teddy Bear Song\" that year with another hit, \"Kid Stuff,\" which reached number 2 on the country chart,<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> and made the [[Billboard Hot 100|''Billboard'' Hot 100]] at No. 95.{{Citation needed|date=September 2019}} In 1974, she scored another Top 10 with the song \"Baby Doll.\"<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> Between 1974 and 1977, she had several other successes, including \"Standing In Your Line,\" \"Little Girl Feelin',\" \"[[Mississippi (Pussycat song)|Mississippi]],\" \"Cheatin' Is,\" and \"Let Me Love You Once Before You Go.\"<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/>\n\n===Later career and life===\nShe turned her attention to [[Gospel music]], where she has recorded both solo and with [[Connie Smith]] and [[Sharon White (singer)|Sharon White]] on the album, ''[[Love Never Fails (Connie Smith album)|Love Never Fails]]''. She now lives in [[Branson, Missouri|Branson]], Missouri, with her husband Roy Morris, who is also a singer-songwriter.<ref name=music/>\n\n== Discography ==\n{{Main|Barbara Fairchild discography}}\n\n==Awards and honors==\n*Inducted into the Christian Music Hall of Fame in 2009.\n*Nominated for Christian Country Female Vocalist of the Year for a Visionary Award by the Christian Music Hall of Fame.\n\n==References==\n{{Reflist}}\n\n==External links==\n{{commons category|Barbara Fairchild}}\n* {{Official|http://www.barbarafairchild.com/site/}} <!-- Seems not to have been updated since 2013 -->\n\n{{Barbara Fairchild}}\n{{Authority control}}\n\n{{DEFAULTSORT:Fairchild, Barbara}}\n[[Category:1950 births]]\n[[Category:Living people]]\n[[Category:American country singer-songwriters]]\n[[Category:American women country singers]]\n[[Category:People from Clay County, Arkansas]]\n[[Category:People from Branson, Missouri]]\n[[Category:American gospel singers]]\n[[Category:Singer-songwriters from Missouri]]\n[[Category:Singer-songwriters from Arkansas]]\n[[Category:Country musicians from Missouri]]\n[[Category:Country musicians from Arkansas]]\n[[Category:21st-century American women]]"}]}}}}{"batchcomplete":"","warnings":{"main":{"*":"Subscribe to the mediawiki-api-announce mailing list at <https://lists.wikimedia.org/postorius/lists/mediawiki-api-announce.lists.wikimedia.org/> for notice of API deprecations and breaking changes. This format is deprecated, and in the future the new format will always be used."}},"query":{"normalized":[{"from":"Joe_Bonsall","to":"Joe Bonsall"}],"pages":{"1408068":{"pageid":1408068,"ns":0,"title":"Joe Bonsall","revisions":[{"contentformat":"text/x-wiki","contentmodel":"wikitext","*":"{{Short description|American singer-songwriter (1948\u20132024)}}\n{{Use mdy dates|date=July 2024}}\n{{Infobox musical artist\n| name              = Joe Bonsall\n| image             = Joe Bonsall by Gage Skidmore (cropped).jpg\n| caption           = Bonsall in 2013\n| background        = solo_singer\n| birth_name        = Joseph Sloan Bonsall Jr.\n| alias             = \n| birth_date        = {{birth date|1948|05|18}}\n| birth_place       = [[Philadelphia|Philadelphia, Pennsylvania]], U.S.\n| death_date        = {{death date and age|2024|07|09|1948|05|18}}\n| death_place       = [[Hendersonville, Tennessee]], U.S.\n| genre             = [[Country music|Country]], [[Gospel music|gospel]]\n| occupation        = Singer, author\n| instrument        = \n| years_active      = 1973\u20132024\n| label             = \n| past_member_of    = [[The Oak Ridge Boys]]\n| website           = {{url|www.josephsbonsall.com}}\n}}\n\n'''Joseph Sloan Bonsall Jr.''' (May 18, 1948 \u2013 July 9, 2024) was an American singer who was the tenor vocalist of the [[country music|country]] and [[gospel music|gospel]] vocal quartet [[the Oak Ridge Boys]] from 1973 to 2023.<ref>{{cite web | url=http://www.cmt.com/artists/az/oak_ridge_boys_the/bio.jhtml|website= CMT.com |title=The Oak Ridge Boys : Biography |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110606150925/http://www.cmt.com/artists/az/oak_ridge_boys_the/bio.jhtml | archive-date=June 6, 2011 | url-status=dead}}</ref> Besides charting numerous hits as a member of the Oak Ridge Boys, Bonsall had a solo chart credit alongside the band [[Sawyer Brown]] in their 1986 single \"Out Goin' Cattin'\", on which he was credited as \"Cat Joe Bonsall\".\n\n== Biography ==\nBonsall was born on May 18, 1948 in Philadelphia, to Joseph Sloan Bonsall Sr., and Lillie Maude Collins, who both served in [[World War II]].<ref name=\"bb\"/><ref name=nyt>{{cite web|title=Joe Bonsall, Tenor Voice of the Oak Ridge Boys, Dies at 76|url=https://www.nytimes.com/2024/07/10/arts/music/joe-bonsall-dead.html|date=July 10, 2024|last=Friskics-Warren|first=Bill|work=The New York Times|access-date=July 10, 2024|archive-date=July 10, 2024|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240710220633/https://www.nytimes.com/2024/07/10/arts/music/joe-bonsall-dead.html|url-status=live}}</ref> He also had a sister, Nancy Marie.\n\nBonsall performed in [[gospel music|gospel]] groups of the [[Northeastern United States]] before joining [[the Oak Ridge Boys]] in 1973.<ref name=\"bb\">{{cite magazine |last1=Hurst |first1=Jack |title=The Unlikely Rise And Continuing Reign of the Strangely-Rooted Oaks |url=https://archive.org/details/bub_gb_MiQEAAAAMBAJ/page/n56/mode/1up?q=%22bonsall%22 |magazine=Billboard |access-date=July 10, 2024 |date=October 13, 1984}}</ref> As an author, Bonsall released a four-part children's book series in 1997 titled ''The Molly Books''<ref>{{cite web |title=Oak Ridge Boys |url= http://www.oakridgeboys.com/index.php?option=com_content&view=article&id=100&Itemid=41 |website=www.oakridgeboys.com |access-date=July 8, 2011 |archive-date=July 14, 2011 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110714223710/http://www.oakridgeboys.com/index.php?option=com_content&view=article&id=100&Itemid=41 |url-status=live }}</ref> and in 2003 published ''GI Joe and Lillie'',<ref>The full title of the book is \"G.I. Joe & Lillie: Remembering a Life of Love and Loyalty\" (2003) New Leaf Press {{ISBN|0-89221-537-2}}</ref> a book about his parents' lives during and after [[World War II]].  He also authored On The Road With The Oak Ridge Boys, An American Journey, From My Perspective, and An Inconvenient Christmas.  His latest book, ''I See Myself'', a memoir, is slated to be released posthumously in November 2024.<ref name=\"AP-Obit\" />\n\nBonsall was inducted into the [[Country Music Hall of Fame and Museum|Country Music Hall of Fame]] in 2015 as a member of the Oak Ridge Boys.<ref name=nyt />\n\n=== Illness and death ===\nBonsall missed several tour dates in 2022 and was hospitalized for what at the time was stated to be [[pulmonary embolism]]s;<ref>{{Cite web |last=Watts |first=Cindy |date=September 19, 2023 |title=The Oak Ridge Boys Announce Farewell Tour |url=https://www.cmt.com/news/hkt8uf/the-oak-ridge-boys-announce-farewell-tour |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230928110644/https://www.cmt.com/news/hkt8uf/the-oak-ridge-boys-announce-farewell-tour |url-status=dead |archive-date=September 28, 2023 |access-date=2023-09-21 |website=[[Country Music Television|CMT]] |language=en}}</ref> by 2023, Bonsall developed what he described as \"a bad leg. It doesn't hurt, it's just\u2014bad.\"<ref name=\"savingcountrymusic.com\">[https://www.savingcountrymusic.com/aaron-mccune-keeps-bass-singing-alive-with-oak-ridge-boys-dailey-vincent/ Aaron McCune Keeps Bass Singing Alive with Oak Ridge Boys, Dailey & Vincent]. ''Saving Country Music''. February 3, 2023. Retrieved September 20, 2023.</ref> For the group's farewell tour, Bonsall sang sitting on a stool and had to be assisted on and off the stage by fellow band member Duane Allen.<ref name=\"savingcountrymusic.com\"/> On January 3, 2024, Bonsall released a statement announcing his retirement from touring with the Oak Ridge Boys due to a \"slow-onset [[neuromuscular disease]]\" (posthumously revealed as being [[amyotrophic lateral sclerosis]], ALS) that was diagnosed in 2019. For the rest of the tour, Ben James sang in Bonsall's stead.<ref>{{Cite web |last=Black |first=Lauren Jo |date=January 3, 2024 |title=Joe Bonsall Bids Farewell to The Oak Ridge Boys After Five Decades on the Road |url=https://countrynow.com/joe-bonsall-bids-farewell-to-the-oak-ridge-boys-after-five-decades-on-the-road/ |access-date=January 4, 2024 |website=Country Now |language=en-US |archive-date=July 10, 2024 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240710125542/https://countrynow.com/joe-bonsall-bids-farewell-to-the-oak-ridge-boys-after-five-decades-on-the-road/ |url-status=live }}</ref> \n\nBonsall died due to complications of ALS in [[Hendersonville, Tennessee]], on July 9, 2024, at the age of 76.<ref name=\"AP-Obit\">{{cite news |last1=Sherman {{flatlist|\n*[[Acoustic guitar]]\n*[[banjo]]}}\n| genre             = {{hlist|[[Country music|Country]]|[[Gospel music|gospel]]|[[rockabilly]]|[[Country music|country bop]]}}\n| occupation        = {{hlist|Singer-songwriter|musician|radio and TV host|actor}}\n| years_active      = 1930\u20131968\n| label             = {{hlist|[[American Record Corporation|Conqueror]]|[[Melotone Records (US)|Melotone]]|[[Decca Records|Decca]]}}\n| past_member_of    = {{hlist|[[The Cumberland Valley Boys]]|[[The Sunshine Boys]]|[[Ernest Tubb]]|[[Kitty Wells]]}}\n| website           = [https://web.archive.org/web/20080728101452/http://www.clydefoleycummins.com/RedFoley.php Official website] (archived)\n}}\n\n'''Clyde Julian''' \"'''Red'''\" '''Foley''' (June 17, 1910 \u2013 September 19, 1968)<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\">{{cite book|title=[[Encyclopedia of Popular Music|The Guinness Who's Who of Country Music]]|editor=[[Colin Larkin (writer)|Colin Larkin]]|publisher=[[Guinness Publishing]]|date=1993|edition=First|isbn=0-85112-726-6|pages=140/2}}</ref> was an American musician who made a major contribution to the growth of [[country music]] after World War II.\n\nFor more than two decades, Foley was one of the biggest stars of the genre, selling more than 25 million records. His 1951 hit, \"[[Peace in the Valley]]\", was among the first million-selling [[gospel music|gospel]] records. A [[Grand Ole Opry]] veteran until his death, Foley also hosted the first popular country music series on network television, ''[[Ozark Jubilee]]'', from 1955 to 1960.\n\nHe is a member of the [[Country Music Hall of Fame]], which called him \"one of the most versatile and moving performers of all time\" and \"a giant influence during the formative years of contemporary Country music.\"\n\n==Biography==\nFoley was born on a {{convert|24|acre|ha|adj=on}} farm in [[Blue Lick, Kentucky]],<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> and grew up nearby [[Berea, Kentucky|Berea]]. He gained the nickname Red for his hair color. He was born into a musical family, and by the time he was nine was giving impromptu concerts at his father's general store, playing French harp, piano, banjo, trombone, harmonica and guitar. At 17, he won first prize in a statewide talent show. He graduated from Berea High School, and later worked as a $2-a-show usher and singer at a theater in [[Covington, Kentucky]]. Foley was of [[Irish people|Irish]] ancestry<ref>{{Cite web|url=https://irishamerica.com/2012/01/roots-the-foley-family/|title=Roots: The Foley Family |website=Irishamerica.com|date=January 26, 2012 |access-date=August 6, 2021}}</ref>\n\n===Early radio and recording career===\nIn 1930, as a freshman at [[Georgetown College (Kentucky)|Georgetown College]], Foley was chosen by a talent scout from Chicago's [[WLS (AM)|WLS-AM]] to sing with producer John Lair's Cumberland Ridge Runners, the house band on ''[[National Barn Dance]]''. His first single, \"Life is Good Enough for Me / The Lone Cowboy\", was released in June 1933 on the [[Melotone Records (US)|Melotone]] label. In 1937 he returned to Kentucky with Lair to help establish the ''[[Renfro Valley Barn Dance]]'' stage and radio show near [[Mt. Vernon, Kentucky|Mt. Vernon]] in 1939, performing everything from ballads to [[boogie-woogie]] to [[blues]]. In late 1939, Foley became the first country artist to host a network radio program, [[NBC Red Network|NBC]]'s ''Avalon Time'' (co-hosted by [[Red Skelton]]), and he performed extensively at theaters, clubs and fairs. He then returned for another seven-year stint with ''National Barn Dance''.\n\nIn 1941, the same year he made his first of only two [[Cinema of the United States|film]] appearances (portraying himself) with [[Tex Ritter]] in the [[western (genre)|Western]], ''The Pioneers'', Foley signed a lifetime contract with [[Decca Records]]. He also released \"[[Old Shep (song)|Old Shep]]\" in 1941, a song he wrote with Arthur Willis in 1933 about a dog he owned as a boy (in reality, his [[German shepherd]], poisoned by a neighbor, was named Hoover). The song, later recorded by many artists including [[Hank Snow]] and [[Elvis Presley]], became a country classic.<ref name=\"Red Foley Fans Tribute\">{{cite web|url=http://profile.myspace.com/index.cfm?fuseaction=user.viewprofile&friendid=416559146|title=Red Foley Fans Tribute|website=Myspace.com|access-date=2009-03-14|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20090115031809/http://profile.myspace.com/index.cfm?fuseaction=user.viewprofile|archive-date=January 15, 2009|df=mdy-all}}</ref> His patriotic 1944 single, \"[[Smoke On The Water (Red Foley song)|Smoke on the Water]]\", topped the folk records chart for 13 consecutive weeks, and on January 17, 1945, Foley was the first country performer to record in [[Nashville, Tennessee]]. During the session at [[WSM (AM)|WSM-AM]]'s Studio B, he recorded \"[[Tennessee Saturday Night]]\", \"Blues in My Heart\" and \"Tennessee Border\". He soon became known for such songs as \"The Death of Floyd Collins\" and \"The Sinking of the Titanic\".<ref>The Associated Press (September 21, 1968) \"Red Foley Dead; Country Singer\", ''[[The New York Times]]'', p. 33</ref> He moved to Nashville in 1946 and was briefly a member of the Brown's Ferry Four, recording \"Jesus Hold My Hand\" and \"I'll Meet You in the Morning\".\n\n===Mr. Country Music===\nIn April 1946, Foley signed on to emcee and perform on ''The Prince Albert Show'', the segment of the ''[[Grand Ole Opry]]'' carried on [[NBC Red Network|NBC Radio]]. During the next eight years he established himself as one of the most respected and versatile performers in country music. He acted as master of ceremonies, the straight man for Opry comedians [[Rod Brasfield]] and [[Minnie Pearl]], and proved himself a vocalist who could handle all types of material.<ref name=\"Red Foley Fans Tribute\"/> His popularity was credited with establishing the Opry as America's top country music radio show. In 1949, Foley was part of the Opry's first European tour, visiting U.S. military bases in England, West Germany and the Azores, with Brasfield, [[Roy Acuff]], Minnie Pearl, [[Little Jimmy Dickens]], [[Hank Williams]] and others.<ref>{{cite web|title=History of the Opry|work=Grand Ole Opry|publisher=Gaylord Entertainment|url=http://opry.com/about/index.html|access-date=2010-01-26|archive-date=July 27, 2016|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160727231806/http://www.opry.com/about/index.html|url-status=dead}}</ref>\n\nFoley began recording with his backing group, the Cumberland Valley Boys, in 1947. He recorded seven top five hits with the group between 1947 and 1949, including a No.&nbsp;1 single, \"[[New Jolie Blonde (New Pretty Blonde)]]\" (a cover of a 1946 [[Moon Mullican]] hit), and the country boogie anthem \"[[Tennessee Saturday Night]]\", a chart-topper in 1948. In 1950, he had three million-sellers: \"[[Just a Closer Walk with Thee (song)|Just a Closer Walk with Thee]]\", \"[[Steal Away]]\" (recorded by Hank Williams as \"The Funeral\"), and a solo version of the song that became his trademark, \"[[Chattanoogie Shoe Shine Boy]]\".<ref name=\"The Book of Golden Discs\">{{cite book\n| first= Joseph\n| last= Murrells\n| year= 1978\n| title= The Book of Golden Discs\n| edition= 2nd\n| publisher= Barrie and Jenkins Ltd\n| location= London\n| page= [https://archive.org/details/bookofgoldendisc00murr/page/50 50]\n| isbn= 0-214-20512-6\n| url-access= registration\n| url= https://archive.org/details/bookofgoldendisc00murr/page/50\n}}</ref> Featuring guitarist [[Grady Martin]], it stayed at No.&nbsp;1 on ''[[Billboard magazine|Billboard]]'s'' [[Hot Country Songs|country chart]] for 13 weeks and hit the pop chart as well.\n\nIn April 1951, Foley was pleased when the popular [[Andrews Sisters]] (Patty, Maxene, and LaVerne) flew from Hollywood to Nashville to join him for a two-day recording session, both acts hoping to repeat the previous successes that the sisters enjoyed when they teamed with [[Burl Ives]] in 1947 and [[Ernest Tubb]] in 1949, producing both folk and country hits. While the results proved to be less popular, the ten tunes recorded were vocally well-executed and received a good deal of play on the country radio stations. The songs included the rhythmic \"Satins and Lace\", the rockabilly-flavored novelty \"Where Is Your Wandering Mother Tonight?\", a very slow rendering of the forlorn hillbilly classic \"[[Bury Me Beneath the Willow]]\", two duets by Foley and Patty Andrews, and two country gospel favorites: \"[[It Is No Secret (What God Can Do)]]\" and \"He Bought My Soul at Calvary\".<ref>Sforza, John: \"Swing It! The Andrews Sisters Story;\" University Press of Kentucky, 2000; 289 pages</ref>\n\nIn 1951, Foley's second wife, Judy Martin, took her own life. To devote more time to his family in Nashville, he cut back on performing but continued to release hits in a variety of styles, including [[rockabilly]] and [[rhythm and blues]]. His 1951 hit, [[Peace in the Valley|\"(There'll Be) Peace in the Valley (for Me)\"]] backed by the Sunshine Boys quartet, was one of the first [[gospel music]] records to sell one million copies. He also released his first LP that year, ''Souvenir Album'' (Decca DL-5303).\n\nFoley's manager was Jim McConnell and \"Dub\" Albritton was his personal appearances manager. Starting in 1951, he hosted ''The Red Foley Show'' on Saturday afternoons on [[NBC#Defining radio's golden age|NBC Radio]] from Nashville (moving to [[Citadel Media|ABC Radio]] and [[Springfield, Missouri]] from 1956 to 1961) sponsored by [[Dow Chemical]]. On November 21, 1953, he was one of the first eight singers named to ''Billboard'' magazine's Honor Roll of Country and Western artists, \"named by the disk jockeys of America as an all-time great of country & western music.\"<ref>\"Honor Roll of C&W Artists\" (December 5, 1953) ''[[Billboard (magazine)|The Billboard]]'', p. 48</ref>\n\nFoley never lost his love for country music and, unlike [[Eddy Arnold]], never sought success as a pop artist, even though many of his recordings made the pop charts.<ref name=\"Red Foley Fans Tribute\"/> Other hits included \"[[Sugarfoot Rag]]\", \"Cincinnati Dancing Pig\" and \"[[Birmingham Bounce]]\", which stayed at No.&nbsp;1 for 14 weeks. Foley's success with the song prompted 21 cover versions. \"One By One\", a duet with [[Kitty Wells]], hit No.&nbsp;1 in 1954 and stayed on the charts for 41 weeks. He also recorded with [[Ernest Tubb]] (with whom he maintained a fictitious on-air \"feud\"), the Dixie Dons, the [[Anita Kerr Singers]], [[Rosetta Tharpe]], Evelyn Knight and the [[Lawrence Welk]] Orchestra. Known by then as Mr. Country Music and America's Favorite Country Gentleman, critics dubbed him the \"barnyard [[Bing Crosby]]\".<ref>Dessauer, Phil \"Springfield, Mo.-Radio City of Country Music\" (April 1957), ''[[Coronet (magazine)|Coronet]]'', p. 154</ref>\n\n===Television career===\n[[File:Red Foley.jpg|thumb|right|180px|Foley c. 1956]]After several years in virtual retirement, Foley moved to Springfield, Missouri in July 1954 after music executive [[Si Siman]] convinced him to host ''[[Ozark Jubilee]]'' on [[American Broadcasting Company|ABC-TV]] and [[Citadel Media|radio]]. The deal was made over a bottle of [[Jack Daniel's]] whiskey at the Andrew Jackson Hotel in [[Nashville, Tennessee]].<ref>Sylvester, Ron \"100 Ozarkers: 'Si' Siman Impacted Country Music\" (October 10, 1999), \"Progress,\" ''Springfield News-Leader'', p.&nbsp;8H</ref> Foley struggled with alcohol,<ref>{{Cite book|first=Rita|last=Spears-Stewart|title=Remembering the Ozark Jubilee|publisher=Stewart, Dillbeck & White Productions|year=1993|isbn=0-9638648-0-7}}, p. 13</ref> which according to [[Maxine Brown (country singer)|Maxine Brown]], \"was a well-kept secret among all the entertainers because we loved him so much.\"<ref>{{cite book| author=Brown, Maxine| year = 2005| title=Looking Back to See: A Country Music Memoir| publisher=University of Arkansas Press| location=Fayetteville| isbn =1-55728-790-2}}</ref>\n\nIn 1955, an official act of the [[Oklahoma Legislature]] honored him as the artist who has \"contributed with humility and reverence more than any other person to perpetuate the music so deeply embedded in the hearts of the American people.\"<ref>Sachs, Bill (May 21, 1955) ''The Billboard'', p. 37</ref> That same year, he was credited with discovering 11-year-old [[Brenda Lee]], who became a ''Jubilee'' regular. On the October 4, 1956 program, Decca executives presented him with a [[Music recording sales certification|gold record]] for \"Peace in the Valley\".<ref>Sachs, Bill \"Folk Talent & Tunes\" (September 22, 1956) ''The Billboard'', p. 71.</ref> The ''Jubilee'' ran for nearly six years and further cemented Foley's fame, but was canceled partly because of [[Income tax (U.S.)|federal income tax]] [[Tax avoidance and tax evasion|evasion charges]] pending against him in 1960. His first trial that fall ended with a hung jury, but on April 23, 1961, he was quickly acquitted.<ref>\"Foley Acquitted of Tax Evasion\" (April 23, 1961), ''[[Springfield News-Leader|Springfield Leader & Press]]'', p. A1</ref>\n\nFrom 1954 to 1955, Foley recorded a number of [[Acetate discs|transcriptions]] for [[Ralph D. Foster|RadiOzark Enterprises]] in Springfield with his band of [[Tommy Jackson (musician)|Tommy Jackson]] on fiddle, Grady Martin on guitar, steel guitarist [[Bud Isaacs]], guitarist [[Jimmie Selph]], [[Bob Moore (musician)|Bob Moore]] on bass and Billy Burke on accordion.\n\nOn April 25, 1956, he appeared on ABC's ''[[Masquerade Party]]'' as Little Red Riding Hood, and made a guest appearance the next day on CBS' ''[[Strike It Rich (radio-TV)|Strike it Rich]]''.<ref>Sachs, Bill \"Folk Talent & Tunes\" (April 28, 1956) ''The Billboard'', p. 58.</ref> He appeared on ABC's ''[[The Pat Boone Chevy Showroom]]'', a 1957\u20131960 program hosted by his son-in-law [[Pat Boone]]. On February 22, 1960, he appeared on ''[[Tonight Starring Jack Paar]]''.<ref>\"Talent on the Road\" (February 22, 1960), ''The Billboard'', p. 53.</ref> In the summer of 1961, Foley appeared twice on NBC-TV's ''[[Five Star Jubilee]]'' and made 58 appearances at 22 state fairs with [[Uncle Cyp and Aunt Sap Brasfield|Boob Brasfield]].<ref>\"Red Foley Set on 58 Fair Dates\" (July 3, 1961), ''Billboard'', p. 9</ref> He moved to Los Angeles, and from 1962 to 1963 played [[Fess Parker]]'s Uncle Cooter on ''[[Mr. Smith Goes to Washington (TV series)|Mr. Smith Goes to Washington]]'', an ABC-TV sitcom. In 1963, he returned to Nashville and performed and toured with the Grand Ole Opry. He appeared in the 1966 film musical, ''Sing a Song, for Heaven's Sake'', and was a guest on ''[[The Joey Bishop Show (talk show)|The Joey Bishop Show]]'' on August 24, 1967.\n\nFoley was elected to the [[Country Music Hall of Fame]] in 1967 (the first Kentuckian and one of only six then-living inductees), which honored him as \"one of the most versatile and moving performers of all time\" and \"a giant influence during the formative years of contemporary Country music and today a timeless legend.\"\n\n===Death===\nOn September 19, 1968, Foley appeared in two Opry performances in [[Fort Wayne, Indiana]], sponsored by the local Sheriff's Posse<ref>\"Red Foley, a Country Great, is Found Dead\" September 1968 ''Billboard''</ref> that included [[Billy Walker (musician)|Billy Walker]] and 19-year-old [[Hank Williams, Jr.]], son of his long-time friend [[Hank Williams]]. Williams Jr. noted that Foley was somewhat slower than usual that day and had no appetite. Before the second show, according to Walker, Foley came to his dressing room and Walker shared his faith in [[Jesus|Christ]]: Foley said, \"Do you think God could ever forgive a sinner like me?\" He began to tell me all the rotten things he had done in his life, and I looked him in the face and said, \"Red, if God can forgive me, He can forgive you.\" I prayed with Red. He went out, and the last song he sang was \"Peace in the Valley\". He came over to side of the stage and said, 'Billy, I've never sung that song and feel the way I do tonight.'<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.billywalker.com|title=In Memory of Billy Walker|publisher=The estate of Billy Walker|access-date=2009-04-17}}</ref> Foley suffered [[acute pulmonary edema|respiratory failure]] that night and died in his sleep, prompting Hank Williams, Jr. to write and record (as [[Luke the Drifter#Luke the Drifter|Luke the Drifter]], Jr.) \"I Was With Red Foley (The Night He Passed Away\").<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> According to the song, which charted that November, his [[last words]] were, \"I'm awful tired now, Hank. I've got to go to bed.\"<ref name=\"LarkinCountry\"/> Foley had sung \"Peace in the Valley\" at Hank Sr.'s funeral. Foley was interred in [[Woodlawn Memorial Park (Nashville, Tennessee)|Woodlawn Memorial Park]] in Nashville.\n\n==Family==\nFoley had an older brother, Clarence \"Cotton\" Foley (1903\u20131988), who in 1939, along with brother Red, John Lair, and [[The Duke of Paducah|Whitey Ford]], co-founded the [[Renfro Valley Barn Dance]] in Rockcastle County, Kentucky.\n\nFoley's first wife was Axie Pauline Cox, who died giving birth to their daughter Betty. Betty (1933\u20131990) married Bentley Cummins in 1948 and had three children. On August 9, 1933, Foley married his second wife, Eva Alaine Overstake. Known during her solo career as [[Judy Martin (singer)|Judy Martin]], she was one of the Three Little Maids on ''National Barn Dance'' and a sister of country music songwriter [[Jenny Lou Carson]]. Red and Eva had three daughters.\n\nOn November 17, 1951, Eva Foley died by suicide.<ref>{{cite book |last1=Rogers & Langley |title=The Life and Times of Jenny Lou Carson |date=2005 |publisher=Nova/Nashville Books |isbn=0-9628452-4-8 |page=236}}</ref>\n\nOn December 17, 1952, Foley announced in Nashville that he had secretly married his third wife, radio and TV entertainer Sally Sweet, on October 28 in [[Iuka, Mississippi]].<ref>AP Wirephoto pc406124tsn caption \"Secretly Married\", (December 17, 1952)</ref> Earlier that year, he had settled out of court with Sweet's former husband, Nashville music publisher Frank B. Kelton, who had sued him in April for $100,000 for alienation of affection.<ref>\"'Red' Foley Weds\" (December 17, 1952) Danville, Va. ''Bee'', p. B9</ref>\n\nShirley Lee Foley married actor-singer [[Pat Boone]] in 1953. Their daughters include [[Cherry Boone]] and [[Debby Boone]].\n\n==Legacy==\nFoley was an inspiration to rock 'n' roll, in particular [[Jerry Lee Lewis]] and [[Elvis Presley]], who both covered many of his songs. His country boogie material was a clear precursor of the style.\n\nFoley has two stars on the [[Hollywood Walk of Fame]]: one for his recording career at, 6225 Hollywood Blvd. and one for his television career, at 6300 Hollywood Blvd. On June 10, 2003, a Kentucky State [[historical marker]] (No.&nbsp;2114) was placed at Foley's boyhood home in Berea.\n\nIn 2002, he was inducted into the Kentucky Music Hall of Fame, where his corncob pipe is on display. In [[List of recordings preserved in the United States National Recording Registry#2006|2006]], his 1951 version of \"Peace in the Valley\" was entered into the [[Library of Congress]]' [[National Recording Registry]].<ref>{{Cite web|url=https://www.loc.gov/programs/national-recording-preservation-board/about-this-program/|title=About This Program &#124; National Recording Preservation Board &#124; Programs at the Library of Congress|website=Loc.gov|access-date=January 25, 2020}}</ref>\n\nIn 1970, [[Berea College]] established the Red Foley Memorial Music Award. Initiated by his long-time friend and colleague Si Siman, the annual award is presented to talented Berea College students in recognition of their musical contributions to the campus community. It is intended to promote the music associated with Foley's career, such as [[folk music|folk]], country, [[Bluegrass music|bluegrass]], gospel and popular music.\n\nA dance to Foley's recording of \"Papa\" John Gordy's song, The [[Salty Dog Rag]], has been [[Dartmouth College traditions|traditional at Dartmouth College]] since 1972, where it is taught to freshmen during orientation.<ref>{{Cite web|url=http://2010.dartmouth.edu/beinga10/traditions/|archiveurl=https://archive.today/20120709020452/http://2010.dartmouth.edu/beinga10/traditions/|url-status=dead|title=Traditions|archive-date=July 9, 2012|access-date=August 6, 2021}}</ref><ref>{{Cite web|url=https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=tCrIKSIvTKI |archive-url=https://ghostarchive.org/varchive/youtube/20211213/tCrIKSIvTKI |archive-date=2021-12-13 |url-status=live|title=Dartmouth\u2013The Salty Dog Rag|date=October 11, 2008 |publisher=[[YouTube]]|access-date=January 25, 2020}}{{cbignore}}</ref>\n\nThe Rooks Van Dellen Residence Hall at [[Calvin College]] has an annual celebration of Red Foley Day in mid-November. The celebration began in 1968 when a few students from the hall heard a radio announcer say, \"Red Foley was a great country music singer, too bad no one will ever remember him.\" The day has been celebrated (with a few lapses) to remember Foley ever since.<ref>{{Cite web|url=https://calvin.edu/directory/places/rooks-van-dellen-hall|title=Rooks-Van Dellen Hall|website=Calvin.edu|access-date=January 25, 2020}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.facebook.com/group.php?gid=144816492212385&v=info |title=Facebook |website=Facebook.com |access-date=2013-07-02}}</ref>\n\nFoley Middle School, located in Berea near Foley's boyhood home, opened in 1978 and educates students in southern Madison County in the 6th, 7th, and 8th grades. The school retains a large collection of personal items once owned by Foley on display in the library. The collection was donated by members of his family.\n\nRed Foley Court is among several streets in [[Nixa, Missouri]], named for performers on ''Ozark Jubilee'', and Red Foley Road is in [[Rockcastle County, Kentucky]], north of Renfro Valley.<ref>{{cite web | url = https://maps.google.com/ | title = Google Maps | publisher = Google Maps | access-date = 2009-10-20}}</ref>\n\nIn 2009, singer/songwriter James Power released a song based on Foley's second wife, Eva Overstake (aka Judy Martin), entitled {{YouTube|TLC6yUMidf4|\"A Cowgirl's Suicide\"}}.<ref>{{YouTube|TLC6yUMidf4|A Cowgirl's Suicide}} dedicated to Red Foley's wife Eva Overstake.</ref> The song was featured in the independent film ''Laid Off'' by director John Launchi.\nHis grandson Clyde Foley Cummins is in the music industry as well. He plays numerous instruments and has played in several benefits over the years.\n\n==Discography==\n{{Main|Red Foley discography}}\n\n==Notes==\n{{Reflist}}\n\n==References==\n*The Associated Press (September 21, 1968) \"Red Foley Dead; Country Singer\", ''[[The New York Times]]'', p.&nbsp;33\n*{{cite book| author=Brown, Maxine| year = 2005| title=Looking Back to See: A Country Music Memoir| publisher=University of Arkansas Press| location=Fayetteville| isbn =1-55728-790-2}}\n*\"Foley Acquitted of Tax Evasion\" (April 23, 1961), ''[[Springfield News-Leader|Springfield Leader & Press]]'', p.
